# Advanced Laravel

> As an open-source web application framework, Laravel offers you expressive, elegant syntax and an enjoyable creative experience. But how can you make the most of its features? In this course, full-stack web developer and tech educator Shruti Balasa guides you through advanced concepts in Laravel. Go over how to establish project requirements and set up your project. Learn how to create user roles,

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805) | 3h 14m | Advanced | 12K learners

>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=5)** This includes installing and setting up Laravel, creating models, working with views and controllers, running migrations, basic Eloquent features, and writing CRUD operations for a resource.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=18)** I also assume that you have a basic understanding of PHP, and object-oriented programming, as these are essential skills for any Laravel developer.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=27)** If you're new to Laravel, or haven't worked with it extensively, I suggest taking the beginner level course first.

> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=38)** You can always come back to this course once you've acquired the necessary skills.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=42)** I also recommend having a dedicated computer or a laptop with a good internet connection to practice and build your own project while you follow along the course.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/prerequisites-for-the-course?u=76281980&t=52)** If you're all set, let's get started.

#### What's new in Laravel 10?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's dive into what's new in Laravel 10.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=3)** Firstly, it's important to note that the minimum PHP version for Laravel 10 is now 8.1.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=10)** This means that if you're using PHP 8.0 or lower, you'll need to upgrade your PHP version before creating a Laravel 10 app.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=18)** I'll be using Sail with Docker as my local dev environment throughout this course so I don't need to upgrade my PHP version.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=25)** But if you prefer using any other tool like Homestead, Valet, XAMPP, Laragon, or local Apache server, you might need to upgrade first.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=33)** Let me open the terminal.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=35)** I have just installed a new Laravel application using Sail.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=39)** Let me start Sail in the background using the command 'sail up dash D.'
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=47)** All right, Sail is running.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=49)** Now, I'll open the project in the browser.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=52)** Here it is. A fresh new homepage for Laravel 10.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=57)** This looks really nice.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=58)** All right, let's talk about the changes in the code base.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=62)** One of the major changes in Laravel 10 is the switch from Webpack to Vite.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=67)** This change actually happened in the version 9.19 itself.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=71)** With Vite, you can expect faster builds and better development experience.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=77)** So you'll no longer see the webpack.mix.js.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=81)** Instead, in the new version, we have the Vite configuration.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=85)** If you're migrating from an earlier version of Laravel, you'll need to upgrade your package.json file to use Vite instead of Webpack.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=93)** And so now, you don't have to load your CSS and JS files manually.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=97)** For example, go to Resources, Views, and welcome.blade.php.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=103)** Earlier, we had to load our styles and scripts from the Assets folder, but now, we can instead use the Vite Blade directive and load our resources like so.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=118)** Resources slash CSS slash app dot CSS, and then, the JS resource right here.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=127)** This is one of the major changes.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=130)** Next new feature in Laravel 10 is native type declarations.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=136)** Let me go to App, Console, Kernel.php.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=141)** You will now notice the specification for the return type for every single function throughout the app.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=148)** This makes your code more readable and less prone to errors.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=151)** For example, here's the same Kernel class of an older version.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=155)** Notice that in the older version, the DocBlock suggested the return type and the type of the parameters.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=162)** But in the new version, that's not required because the function signature itself suggests the types.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=168)** This is a big change, but it's backward compatible with existing applications, so you don't have to worry about it.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=176)** Another useful feature in Laravel 10 is the generator CLI prompts.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=180)** Let me show you in the terminal.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=184)** To create a controller, we use the command 'sail artisan make:controller' followed by the name of the controller, for example, user controller.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=195)** And then, if we need a resource controller, we use the dash dash resource flag.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=200)** But now, version 10 onwards, you can simply say make:controller, and let's see what happens.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=207)** You will be prompted for input.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=210)** What should the controller be named?
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=212)** Let's say PostController.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=215)** And then, it asks you which type of controller would you like?
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=219)** Let's say Resource.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=221)** And what model should this resource controller be for?
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=224)** Let's just leave that blank.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=226)** And now, a resource controller is created successfully.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=230)** Check it out.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=232)** So you don't have to remember any options for any of the make commands anymore.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=237)** Amazing, right?
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=239)** And there are a few more advanced features in this version, some of which I will cover later and some more are out of the scope of this course.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=247)** That's it.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-s-new-in-laravel-10?u=76281980&t=248)** Let's make the best of these new features while we learn about the existing ones in depth.

> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/using-the-exercise-files?u=76281980&t=18)** For example, the branch 02-03 has the code that you will see in chapter two, video three.

> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/using-the-exercise-files?u=76281980&t=34)** If you're watching chapter two, video three, the branch named 02-03b contains the code the way it is at the beginning of the video, and the code at the end of the video is in the branch named 02-03e.

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/using-the-exercise-files?u=76281980&t=63)** And if you want to refer to the file within the routes directory named web.php, you can view the code right here, compare it with yours, and copy it if needed, and continue with the course once you fixed it.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/using-the-exercise-files?u=76281980&t=78)** Since we are learning about advanced features, I might skip writing some basic code during some of the videos to save time.

### 1. Starting Your Project

#### Understand project requirements
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We are now going to build a MyGym web application, it's an app for scheduling and booking gym classes.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=7)** This app will have three types of users, instructors, members, and admins.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=14)** Instructors will be able to schedule classes by selecting the type of class, a date, and a time.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=24)** They can view all their upcoming classes and cancel them if they wish to.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=30)** Members will be able to view all the scheduled classes, select and book a slot for themselves.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=38)** They can then view all their upcoming bookings and cancel, if needed.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=43)** Something like this.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=46)** And it's canceled.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=48)** And in case an instructor goes to the upcoming classes and cancels a class, all the members who made a booking will immediately receive a mail saying, this class was canceled.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=63)** Admins should be able to create users, deactivate membership, et cetera, but we will not be implementing any admin features in this course.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=72)** To build this application, we'll use some authorization features in built-in Laravel, like custom middleware, gates and policies.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=80)** We will use advanced eloquent features including many-to-many relationships eager loading, and scoping queries.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=86)** While building this app, we'll also learn about creating events and listeners, sending user notifications, creating jobs, and queuing them.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/understand-project-requirements?u=76281980&t=95)** Lastly, we'll learn about testing and go deeper into Laravel architecture concepts.
>

> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=163)** All right, so back in the handle method, let's check if the authenticated user role is same as the route's role.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=172)** If it's not, then let's abort with a code like 403, forbidden, else, move on to the next step.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=183)** This should work. Let's try.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=185)** Let me log in as an instructor again and let me try to access the member dashboard and see, we're forbidden.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=198)** Let's try to access the admin dashboard and we are forbidden again.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=202)** We can take this one step further and instead of aborting here, we can redirect to the common dashboard route.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=212)** Let me remove this.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=215)** So if we redirect to the common dashboard route, from there, accordingly, the user will be redirected to his or her own dashboard.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=223)** So let's verify this.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=226)** So yes, you'll see if I try to access a member dashboard, I'm redirected back to my own dashboard.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-custom-middleware?u=76281980&t=234)** So now, for all the routes that we want to restrict to a particular role, we can simply add the role middleware with the role value and it will be done.

#### Write policies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A while ago, we implemented the feature of canceling a ScheduledClass.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=5)** Before actually deleting the model, we checked if the authorized user is the one deleting it.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=10)** This ensures that the instructor can cancel only his or her class and not another user's.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=16)** Instead of checking it this way, let's write a policy for the same.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=21)** The first step is to create a policy for the ScheduledClass model, sail artisan make:Policy ScheduledClassPolicy.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=36)** This allows us to organize all related authorization rules in one place.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=41)** We have the Policy class.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=44)** Next we need to register it, but Laderman is capable of automatically discovering policies if you follow the standard naming convention; that is, the policy name must match the name of the model ScheduledClass and the suffix should be Policy.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=62)** Also, this class must be within the Policies directory within app.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=67)** As long as you follow these conventions, you don't have to register it, but otherwise, for some reason, if you don't follow them, you can manually register the policy in AuthServiceProvider, right here, this way.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=82)** Okay, now back to the Policy class.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=85)** In here, we can add methods for each action that we want to authorize on the ScheduledClass model; that is, for the delete action, we can say public function delete, accept the User model first, and then the model for which we are creating the policy; that is the ScheduledClass model.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=110)** Let me type in ScheduledClass, scheduleClass, and then return true if this user can delete the scheduledClass.

> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-policies?u=76281980&t=132)** That means the user can delete it.

>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/implement-eager-loading?u=76281980&t=323)** So when you use this relation that's when Laravel actually makes the query.

> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/implement-eager-loading?u=76281980&t=328)** That means in Laravel, relations are lazy loaded.

> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/implement-eager-loading?u=76281980&t=332)** This causes the n-plus one problem we just saw.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/implement-eager-loading?u=76281980&t=335)** That is one query to get n-rows and then n-quiries to get the relation data.

> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/implement-eager-loading?u=76281980&t=341)** We can use eager loading to avoid this problem.

#### Attaching and detaching relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/attaching-and-detaching-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/attaching-and-detaching-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's implement the booking feature completely.

#### Write complex queries and create query scopes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Shruti] Let's book this class once again.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=2)** Class is booked, great, but if you come back to the list of classes you'll still see this class appearing.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=9)** The member shouldn't be able to see this once it's booked, right?
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=12)** So we need to edit the query to remove booked classes from this list.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=18)** In other words, for each scheduled class we want to check the list of booked members and remove that row if our user ID is found meaning we have to remove that scheduled class if the relationship exists.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=32)** Eloquent allows us to query the relations using various methods like has, where has, where doesn't have, and so on.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=40)** You can go to the documentation, search for querying relationship existence, right here.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=52)** And then you can also look for querying relationship absence.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=58)** In our case right now, we need to query relationship absence because we need those classes which are not booked by the authenticated user.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=66)** That means where the relation does not exist.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=70)** So back to the code.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=73)** So change this query with where doesn't have pass the relationship members.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=82)** And we cannot leave it just here.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=84)** We have to add another constraint because if we don't then all the scheduled classes with at least one member will be excluded where it doesn't have members, right?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-complex-queries-and-create-query-scopes?u=76281980&t=95)** But we only want to exclude those where the user ID is our authenticated user.

>

#### Send notifications
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/send-notifications?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/send-notifications?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] What we sent just now is in fact a notification.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/send-notifications?u=76281980&t=4)** Laravel provides support for sending notifications across a variety of delivery channels, including email, SMS, and Slack as well at the same time.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/send-notifications?u=76281980&t=13)** You can even store the notifications in the database and display them within your app.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/send-notifications?u=76281980&t=18)** In this video, we will explore the email channel and send the same class canceled email using notifications instead.

### 6. Testing

#### Get started with testing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Testing is an essential part of software development.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=3)** It allows you to ensure that your code is working as expected and that any changes you make won't break existing functionality.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=11)** If you're new to testing, don't worry.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=13)** We'll be covering the basics in the next few videos.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=16)** When you install Laravel, the testing framework PHPUnit is included out of the box.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=21)** You don't have to do any additional configuration to start testing right away.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=27)** You may have noticed a test directory in the root of your application.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=32)** This includes two different directories for feature tests and unit tests.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=37)** Unit tests are for testing small units of the application, usually individual methods in isolation.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=44)** Feature tests are for slightly more complex tests that might cover an entire feature.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=49)** Generally, most of your tests should be feature tests.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-started-with-testing?u=76281980&t=52)** These type of tests provide the most confidence that your system as a whole is functioning as intended.

#### Write database tests
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] While writing HTTP tests, we have used some database-related utilities like model factories and seeding in our tests.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=8)** In this video, we'll explore additional techniques provided by Laravel.

>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=38)** That means when the test ran for the first time, a class was scheduled for that date and time and that row still exists in the testing database table.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=46)** We won't be able to test multiple times if that happens, right?
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=50)** So Laravel lets us reset the database for each test by using a trait.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=57)** Scroll up here and say use RefreshDatabase, and that's it.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=64)** Now every time you run a test, a database is reset.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=69)** That means none of the records will be stored.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=72)** So now let's try and run the same thing again.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/write-database-tests?u=76281980&t=77)** Perfect, now it passes.
>

#### Test-driven development
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/test-driven-development?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/test-driven-development?u=76281980&t=9)** It's a process where you write tests even before you write the code.

### 7. Understand How Laravel Works

#### Request lifecycle overview
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When a user makes a request to a Laravel application, there are a series of steps that take place before the response is returned to the user.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=9)** This is what we call a request lifecycle.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=12)** With a clear understanding of this flow, you could easily debug issues and optimize the performance of your application.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=18)** So let's understand what Laravel does under the hood.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=22)** The moment a user visits any URL of your app, the web server is configured to point to the public directory as the entry point for all requests.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=32)** Let's look at the code of the Laravel application.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=35)** This is the public directory, and here is the index.php file.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=41)** The first thing Laravel does is it sets the constant LARAVEL_START to the current time in milliseconds.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=48)** This is used to record the start time of the request processing.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=51)** Remember we saw the load time for each request in Telescope?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=55)** This constant is used in such cases to calculate the elapsed time for processing a request.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=61)** Next thing, Laravel checks if the application is in maintenance mode.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=66)** That means it checks the presence of this particular file: storage, framework, maintenance.php.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=75)** We don't have any such file.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=77)** If a file exists, it requires it, and then the application is put into maintenance mode so that the user cannot access any part of it.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=85)** Right now, our application is not in maintenance mode, which is why we cannot see this file.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=91)** Next, we require the auto-load file generated by Composer.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=97)** This auto-loads all the PHP classes and functions from external libraries without needing to manually include the files.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=105)** Now comes the most important part of the application.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=108)** This file, app.php within bootstrap, is required, and the response is stored in this app variable.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's see what this file does.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=118)** Let me open app.php within bootstrap.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=123)** This line, right here, is creating a new application instance.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=129)** This application class is the heart of your entire Laravel project.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=136)** It provides the core functionality for managing service providers, facades, routes, and middleware.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=142)** Routes and middleware you already know, service providers and facades, we will see in the next few videos.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=149)** This application class also sets up the configuration, error handling, and logging for the application, and so much more.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=156)** Basically, this is what is responsible for bootstrapping the application and loading all the required components.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=163)** And then, down here, Laravel binds some important interfaces so that we can resolve them later when required.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=171)** Http\Kernel interface is responsible for handling HTTP requests and sending responses, while the Console\Kernel handles the same for your console commands.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=182)** The ExceptionHandler is responsible for handling errors and exceptions and presenting them to the user in a friendly and informative way.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=190)** Here Laravel is registering singletons of all these interfaces and classes.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=196)** Singletons are nothing but objects that are created only once and then shared across the entire application.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=202)** So even if you try to create an object again, you get the same instance.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=206)** Back in the index.php, here we are creating an instance of the app Http\Kernel class that we just registered right here.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=217)** This one.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=219)** This Kernel class is the one responsible for handling incoming requests, routing them to the appropriate controllers, and returning responses.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=226)** And then, we are calling the handle method on the kernel.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=231)** Now, let's try and find this handle method so we'll understand what's going on.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=236)** And since this is an instance of the app Http\Kernel class, we should be able to find the handle method here.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=245)** And if it's not here, then it must be in the Http\Kernel class that it extends.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=251)** So go into that.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=253)** Find the handle method.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=256)** And yes, right here it is.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=259)** This is the main line of code here, sendRequestThroughRouter.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=264)** Let's see what happens within this.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=267)** And here, these two lines of code make the request variable and the request facade available throughout the application.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=276)** This line next, bootstrap, does certain initializing, connections, configurations, etcetera.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=283)** And finally, this Pipeline class is responsible for sending the request through multiple middleware and then dispatching to the router.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=293)** You already know what middleware does, we've written our own custom middleware.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=298)** It takes the request, adds some additional headers, or authorizes, and so on, and then passes it on to the next middleware, and so on, until finally it is dispatched to the router.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=311)** Let's again go into this method and see what happens.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=315)** This dispatchToRouter method finds the matching route based on the request method, like get, post, delete, and the request URI, and then calls the appropriate controller method as defined in our web.php routes.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=331)** And then you already know how we return views within controller methods, which are Blade files.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=337)** Laravel compiles the Blade code into standard PHP code that can be executed by the server.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=343)** Server then generates the HTML response that is sent back to the client, and that's what you see in the browser.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=350)** That's a simplified overview of the request lifecycle.

> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/request-lifecycle-overview?u=76281980&t=353)** In the next few videos, we'll dive deeper and learn more about what goes on within.

#### What is a service container?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A while ago we saw that this application class is the heart of the entire application.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=5)** This is a subclass of Laravel's service container.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=9)** This service container is responsible for managing the dependencies of an application.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=14)** But first, let's see why we need something called a service container.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=19)** When building any application, you will often find yourself needing to create objects that depend on other objects.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=27)** For example, you might need to create a controller that requires a database connection or an email service.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=33)** Traditionally, you would create these objects manually passing in all their dependencies as arguments.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=40)** Something like this where you create a mailer object manually in the controller, in the controller's constructor.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=49)** This can quickly become too long and error-prone if you have too many dependencies and as your application grows in size and complexity.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=58)** Instead of creating the mailer object manually like this, what if you could do this?
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=64)** If you could just specify the mailer class as a dependency of the controller and somehow an object gets created only when you need to use it, that's exactly what a service container does.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=77)** It's like a central box that can be accessed from anywhere in your application.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=82)** You can tell a service container how to create an object in a different part of the globe.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=88)** Think of this like putting something into the container and later, whenever you need that object like our mailer in whichever part of your application, you can simply access that object without creating it again manually.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=104)** Let's see how the service container does this.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=108)** This is a super simplified version of any service container.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=112)** At the core, a service container simply is a PHP class that provides two main methods, bind and make.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=122)** The bind method takes a key and a closure function, and just stores them in this bindings area.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=130)** And then the main method takes a key, finds the bindings array for that key, gets the closure, and then calls that closure with the container instance as an argument.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=144)** And the result object is return.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=148)** I can make use of this container like soap.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=152)** Container equals new container.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=159)** I create a new instance of the container itself.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=162)** Let's bind a string called greeting and pass the closure function container return hello world.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=179)** This is done once.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=181)** And then whenever we need, we can use the make method to retrieve the value.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=186)** So we can anywhere say greeting equals container make greeting.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=195)** Container calls the closure function associated with this key and returns the resulting string value.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=203)** So in some ways, the service container in Laravel can be thought of as an array that stores bindings between a class and its implementation, but it's much more powerful than that.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=214)** Look at the web dot php routes file.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=219)** In this get route, we have a reference to the dashboard controller.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=222)** Laravel uses the service container to resolve an instance of this dashboard controller class on its own because we've never used a bind method to bind this class, right?
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=232)** So, Laravel discovers and automatically does all of this.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=236)** And suppose we have another dependency within the dashboard controller, public function construct.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=247)** And let's say we have some class, some object dependency, which we are later using it here.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=257)** Laravel will resolve this also recursively while resolving the dashboard controller class.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=264)** This allows for easily managing dependencies, less chaotic code, and also provides a way to change the implementations without changing code in too many places.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/what-is-a-service-container?u=76281980&t=275)** In summary, the service container is a critical component of Laravel framework, providing a way to manage dependencies and resolve objects throughout the application.

#### Service providers in Laravel
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Now that we know how a service container in Laravel manages the objects and their dependencies, we can look at something called service providers.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=10)** A service provider is a class that is responsible for registering the services or dependencies for your application.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=17)** So service provider is a place where you do the binding part of using the service container.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=24)** The core Laravel framework provides several built-in service providers that register various services for you, such as routing, database, validation, and encryption.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=35)** Let's take a look at one of the core service providers in the Laravel.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=39)** The ViewServiceProvider.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=40)** This service provider is responsible for registering the view component of Laravel, which is used to render the views in your application.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=48)** Look at the main register method that calls the registerFactory method.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=53)** It registers the view component with the service container by binding it to the view key.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=60)** So this is what happens when we return a view in our controllers.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=65)** Similar to this ViewServiceProvider, Laravel has several of them responsible for bootstrapping the entire application by registering all of the necessary services and dependencies.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=75)** When your application starts up, Laravel automatically loads all of the service providers you can find in the config app.php.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=86)** Here's all of them that it registers on boot.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/service-providers-in-laravel?u=76281980&t=90)** You can create your own service provider if you like to manage certain dependencies in a clean and organized way, and add them right here.

#### Get to know Facades
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now, let's talk about facades and how they work with the service container in Laravel.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=5)** You have heard about facades and used several of them already.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=8)** Mail facade, notification facade, auth, request, and so on.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=13)** So, what exactly are these?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=15)** In simple words, facades provide a simple and easy to use interface to access the services registered within the Laravel service container.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=25)** Whatever you put into the service container is like a box, right?
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=28)** You can now take out and actually make use of it using facades.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=33)** Let's take an example to understand this better.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=37)** Look at the registered user controller, which is created for us when we install Breeze.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=43)** Scroll down, and here, we use the hash facade to hash passwords and store them in the database.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=50)** Notice that we're not creating an instance of the hash class anywhere here, nor are we manually injecting into our class or function.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=59)** Instead, we're simply calling the static make method on the hash facade.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=64)** But how does Laravel know which service to instantiate when we call the hash facade?
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=69)** Remember, I mentioned that Laravel loads all of the service providers you can find in the config/app.php file.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=77)** Well, HashServiceProvider is one of them.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=80)** Take a look in this.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=84)** Here's the register method of the service provider.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=87)** The hash service is registered as a singleton with the service container.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=93)** So, whenever we call the hash facade, Laravel results the hash service from the service container, and calls the relevant method inside Hash Manager.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=104)** In Hash Manager, let's search for the make method, and yeah, here it is.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=110)** So, this is what is called when you simply say "hash::make" using the facade.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=117)** So now, you know how the service container is responsible for managing object creation and dependencies, while service providers allow for the registration and booting of the application services and components.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=129)** Facades provide a simple and easy to use interface for accessing those services registered.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/advanced-laravel-22373805/get-to-know-facades?u=76281980&t=135)** I hope you now have a much better understanding of what happens in Laravel under the hood.
